  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

  <span id="freeText13649418192135094209" style="display:none">Clad ln a doeskin, alone and unafraid, she stood straight and proud before the onrushlng Forces of America’s destiny: Sacajawea. child of a Shoshoni chief, lone woman on Lewis and Clark‘s historic trek-beautiful spear of a dying nation.<br /><br />She knew many men, walked many miles. From the whispering prairies, across the Great Divide to the crystal-capped Rockies and on to the emerald promise of the Pacific Northwest, her story overflows with emotion and action ripped from the bursting fabric of a raw new land. <br /><br />Ten years In the Writing, SACAJAWEA unfolds an immense canvas of people and events, and captures the eternal longings of a woman who always yearned for one great passion-and always it lay beyond the next mountain.</span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/44674359-gabrielle"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/44674359-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I actually read this book when i was a teenager (late middle school / early high school), and I loved it. <br/><br/>There are definitely some more "adult" theme<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I actually read this book when i was a teenager (late middle school / early high school), and I loved it. <br /><br />There are definitely some more "adult" themes/content, but I don't think it's anything that a teenager shouldn't be able to handle. I think it's the good kind of exposure, to be honest.<br /><br />If I remember correctly she is kidnapped when she is young and forced into a marriage in another tribe, and I believe there is some traumatic (but light) non-consensual sex described.<br /><br />I can't remember all of the specifics, but I do remember that this book had a really good and really profound influence on me. Sacajawea is a strong woman, and reading about her life from start to finish was a great experience for me at the time. <br /><br />So if you haven't let her read it yet....I'd say, yes, she should totally read it.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer423883232" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4783673562822047052">I read this book when I was in 4th and 5th grade. I realize this book was written for adults, but I was obsessed with learning more about Sacajawea at that age. My dad said it would be okay for me to read it, so I did. It took me close to a year to complete the whole thing--but I eventually wrote a 5th grade book report on it when we were assigned to read a historical fiction piece. My teacher rewarded us with a piece of licorice for every 40 pages we read. As you can imagine, with a book over 1</span>
  <span id="freeText4783673562822047052" style="display:none">I read this book when I was in 4th and 5th grade. I realize this book was written for adults, but I was obsessed with learning more about Sacajawea at that age. My dad said it would be okay for me to read it, so I did. It took me close to a year to complete the whole thing--but I eventually wrote a 5th grade book report on it when we were assigned to read a historical fiction piece. My teacher rewarded us with a piece of licorice for every 40 pages we read. As you can imagine, with a book over 1000 pages--I got a lot of licorice. Needless to say, I was VERY popular with my 5th grade classroom the day I delivered my book report.</span>

<div id="review_2151233572" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2151233572" />
    <a title="Karina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62428250-karina"><img alt="Karina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541395389p2/62428250.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2151233572?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karina" href="/user/show/62428250-karina">Karina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2151233572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17470454749997071704">I realize I never wrote a review for this book and it deserves such high praise. Waldo studied Sacajawea's (Shoshone Tribe) life for so long and did such a great job. It is easily in my top 10 books. It was long, 1000 pages, so I wasn't sure if I could handle it but I'm so glad I tried. I couldn't get enough of her life and how brave she was. Pocahontas has nothing on her! <br/><br/>The book is basically about her 8,000 mile journey with the Lewis and Clark expedition as an interpreter of Indian languages</span>
  <span id="freeText17470454749997071704" style="display:none">I realize I never wrote a review for this book and it deserves such high praise. Waldo studied Sacajawea's (Shoshone Tribe) life for so long and did such a great job. It is easily in my top 10 books. It was long, 1000 pages, so I wasn't sure if I could handle it but I'm so glad I tried. I couldn't get enough of her life and how brave she was. Pocahontas has nothing on her! <br /><br />The book is basically about her 8,000 mile journey with the Lewis and Clark expedition as an interpreter of Indian languages. This was a journey that shouldn't have happened bc 1. She was a woman 2. She had just finished giving birth to a baby boy. She marries a horrible Canadian-French man, Toussaint Charbonneau, that mistreats and belittles her but she perseveres and becomes a wonderful, smart historical figure all before feminism was a "thing."<br /><br />I wish historians knew what became of her later life and when and how she died or even what became of her son, Jean "Pomp" Baptiste, but that will always be a mystery. <br /><br />Highly recommend if you are into Native American subject matter. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17470454749997071704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2932944829569747656">Simply put, this is the most amazing story I know and the book is incredibly written. i read this very long book about 14 years ago, and I remember my mother reading it about 10 years prior to that. I still have the actual book that she and I read. It's very special to me, not just for the connection to my mother, but because the story of Sacajawea is so well depicted within it's covers. I remember laughing on one page, then sobbing to the point of having to put the book down on the next. It's d</span>
  <span id="freeText2932944829569747656" style="display:none">Simply put, this is the most amazing story I know and the book is incredibly written. i read this very long book about 14 years ago, and I remember my mother reading it about 10 years prior to that. I still have the actual book that she and I read. It's very special to me, not just for the connection to my mother, but because the story of Sacajawea is so well depicted within it's covers. I remember laughing on one page, then sobbing to the point of having to put the book down on the next. It's depressing, enlightening, romantic, devastating, heart-warming. It's real.<br /><br />I cant wait to read it again and pass it down to my daughter.</span>

<div id="review_2843960473" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2843960473" />
    <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes"><img alt="aPriL does feral sometimes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478243665p2/3409532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2843960473?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="aPriL does feral sometimes " href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=academic-notations">academic-notations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=females-are-denied-rights">females-are-denied-rights</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=illuminating">illuminating</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=letters-diaries-and-journals">letters-diaries-and-journals</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2843960473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6303842849500519305">'Sacajawea' by Anna Lee Waldo is the most detailed historical fiction novel I have ever read. The rumor is it took Waldo ten years to write the novel. Yet, despite that Waldo includes every aspect of life - hunting, building shelters, marriage, raising children, social customs - for various North American Indian tribes between the Mississippi and the Pacific Northwest, she never neglects the inner life of human joy, love and suffering of all of her cast of characters. The personalities are each </span>
  <span id="freeText6303842849500519305" style="display:none">'Sacajawea' by Anna Lee Waldo is the most detailed historical fiction novel I have ever read. The rumor is it took Waldo ten years to write the novel. Yet, despite that Waldo includes every aspect of life - hunting, building shelters, marriage, raising children, social customs - for various North American Indian tribes between the Mississippi and the Pacific Northwest, she never neglects the inner life of human joy, love and suffering of all of her cast of characters. The personalities are each finely drawn and vivid to the eye and heart.<br /><br />Sacajawea was one of the child wives of Toussaint Charbonneau, a 19th-century French-Canadian hunter and trapper reknown for his braggart blowhard speeches, his crude manners and general lazy negligence. Middle-aged Charbonneau treated his twelve- and thirteen-year-old wives like packhorses and slaves - which is why he preferred native girls. For Sacajawea, he was her fourth master. She had been traded to tribe after tribe after she was kidnapped from her murdered family, the Shoshonis (lived in what is now Montana), when she was about ten or eleven, ending up in the Dakotas with the Mandans. She first was raped while enslaved with a Minnetaree tribe. Charbonneau won her when her Mandan owner lost her in a gambling game at a Native-American trading fair. She was thirteen, most likely.<br /><br />Two years later, the Lewis and Clark expedition traveled up the Missouri River, arriving at a trading post where Charbonneau was living. The two explorers asked around for an English-Shoshoni translator. Charbonneau got the job. It apparently was not understood his wife was the Shoshoni translator as Charbonneau claimed her accomplishments for himself. Captain Meriwether Lewis did not want a woman along on the exploratory trek across the new territory America had bought from the French emperor, Napoleon, but he reluctantly gave his assent. Time, and various adventures, soon exposed Charbonneau's true character to the expedition. Everyone soon realized Sacajawea was an enormous asset, knowing the sign language common to many northern and plains tribes, knowing the territory, knowing how to prepare killed animals for food and clothes, knowing the plants which were medicinal or edible. She was smart as hell. Arguably, her biggest contribution was simply being a female with a baby. Yes, a baby. She had had her baby at the beginning of the gig. Tribes saw the armed White men had a woman and a baby with them, and so put their weapons down, thinking no war party would have women and children with them. Charbonneau got the paycheck and the peace medal from President Jefferson, however, when they returned from the Columbia River and the Pacific Ocean beach.<br /><br />The foregoing is not the end of the story. This is what happens to Sacajawea up to page 700. The novel has 1,408 pages in total. <br /><br />When Lewis and Clark return to Washington D.C. to hand in their maps, journals and animal/plant/Indian artifacts, Sacajawea has become aware each Indian tribe treats women differently and has learned the different Indian customs regarding what is right and wrong. Upper-class Lewis and Clark are very different from the rude White and half-breed trappers with whom she has become familiar. Captain William Clark offers Charbonneau a job, land and a paid education for his now family of two boys (one from Sacajawea). Charbonneau finally accepts Clark's offer a few years later, which was still open, after some business matters trapping and interpreting do not go well. In St. Louis, Missouri, Sacajawea learns how White women live.<br /><br />Before Sacajawea's story is over, she spends time with the Comanche, finds true love, learns Spanish in addition to her English, French, Mandon, and other Native-American languages, has more children, meets the Mormons in Utah, and lives through a few more attacks. She saw the famous Mandan ceremony of hanging from a ceiling suspended by skewers inserted under their skin, exactingly described. She endured multiple incidences of starvation, wildfires and awful weather.<br /><br />Wow.<br /><br />While this is a fictionalized biography of the real Sacajawea's life, it is often backed by hard evidence as well as rumors, stories and myths. The hard evidence is collected from many witness journals, letters, and third-hand tales written down in family histories. The author researched many Native-American tribal histories and lore as well as anything mentioning Sacajawea. To call this book in-depth does not do it justice. It is encyclopedic. <br /><br />One of the stories say Sacajawea died at age 24 of a fever, but there is evidence she lived to an old age, scattered as it is. The author assumes Sacajawea lived a long interesting life of adventures, and follows the more opaque written records of her travels after she left the Lewis and Clark expedition.<br /><br />Waldo mostly avoids taking a stance or opinion on cultural customs and beliefs, but she makes obvious what those customs involved. I think she also minimizes the growing knowledge of Native-Americans that their land was being stolen with the resulting aggressive reactions and sorrows. Waldo puts in Sacajawea's mouth late in the book dialogues and thoughts about accepting change and reservation life simply because it was inevitable due to the superior force of many more White people. She notices Native-Americans were beginning to fail as a culture because of the transmission of European diseases to natives, many of which were fatal, the despoiling of animal life and hunting. She is told of one tribe with whom she lived no longer exists. When Sacajawea is old, she sees mountain men, trappers and Indians despairing about all of the civilians moving in.<br /><br />'Sacajawea' is a wonderful book I highly recommend about the American West and the Native-Americans of the early 19th century, before what we now call the Wild West period. This time period is just before and during when many Western native tribes met White men for the first time, just before the years covered wagons and steam engines pulled households across the plains, when buffalo and other types of animal life were plentiful in the warm seasons. However, we readers often romanticize the Indians and the cowboys and the trappers and the mountain men - but I suspect most of us women would not actually want to return to the old social customs expected of women no matter the society. Wow, was it hard being a female in earlier centuries, even when one was a woman whose personal qualities were recognized as being above the common crowd.<br /><br />The book includes an extensive non-fiction fact-based epilogue, a Notes section and a Bibliography.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6303842849500519305" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2359037950" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2359037950" />

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer10383379875752404868">This was, apparently, plagiarized. Avon and Waldo eventually settled out of court and then released an "Expanded Edition" in 1984. So that's fun.<br/><br/>Honestly, it's wild to me that this was such a huge bestseller 40 years ago. It's...not a good book. Expansive, yes; detail-oriented, yes; unique, also yes. Good? No.<br/><br/>There is enough material in <i>Sacajawea</i> for a full trilogy. It's a huge, 1400-page monster. Today, it would be unpublishable. Not only because of its length but because of Waldo's (probably w</span>
  <span id="freeText10383379875752404868" style="display:none">This was, apparently, plagiarized. Avon and Waldo eventually settled out of court and then released an "Expanded Edition" in 1984. So that's fun.<br /><br />Honestly, it's wild to me that this was such a huge bestseller 40 years ago. It's...not a good book. Expansive, yes; detail-oriented, yes; unique, also yes. Good? No.<br /><br />There is enough material in <i>Sacajawea</i> for a full trilogy. It's a huge, 1400-page monster. Today, it would be unpublishable. Not only because of its length but because of Waldo's (probably well-intentioned) racist and supremely harmful depictions of Native Americans. I've no doubt the author really loved Indians, but a white woman can love a culture and still cause harm in a ham-fisted attempt to write about something she could never fully grasp.<br /><br />All in all, only my sheer determination to see this through to the end got me to the epilogue.<br /><br />📌 . <b><a href="https://romanticparvenu.com/blog/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://romanticparvenu.com/reviews/" rel="nofollow noopener">Review Database</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/romanticparvenu" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="https://instagram.com/romanticparvenu" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/4620632-renae" rel="nofollow noopener">Goodreads</a></b></span>

<div id="review_143731896" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143731896" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4877728-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579291918p2/4877728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143731896?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/4877728-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4877728-melissa?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143731896" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17020239895140922810">This book is not for the faint of heart or those who want a quick read. At 1328 pages for just the story and an additional 61 pages of notes this is a titan of a read. But every page is well worth it.<br/><br/>It starts out when Sacajawea is a young girl and covers her capture and enslavement by the Mandan tribe. While with the Mandans she is subjected to rape at around age 11 (the book makes it somewhat hard to pinpoint her age at times), learns the art of glass making, and then is eventually sold off to</span>
  <span id="freeText17020239895140922810" style="display:none">This book is not for the faint of heart or those who want a quick read. At 1328 pages for just the story and an additional 61 pages of notes this is a titan of a read. But every page is well worth it.<br /><br />It starts out when Sacajawea is a young girl and covers her capture and enslavement by the Mandan tribe. While with the Mandans she is subjected to rape at around age 11 (the book makes it somewhat hard to pinpoint her age at times), learns the art of glass making, and then is eventually sold off to another tribe. This tribe is a lot kinder to her and she has a few easy years until she is lost in a wager to her future husband (the perverted Toussaint Charbonneau).<br /><br />We next see Sacajawea pregnant with her first child (John Baptiste also known as Pomp) when she attracts the attention of Lewis and Clark. As her man Charbonneau is to be an interpreter for the expedition, her wit and intelligence cause Clark to ask for her to come along as well. He also reasons that a party traveling with a woman and baby will not look like a war party.<br /><br />Regarding her travels with Lewis and Clark, while the travel west was covered extensively, the return was not given as much detail. Upon their journey they meet several local Indian tribes and the author seems to really hone in that all these people are fond of the native salmon, rotting or fresh, and the character's disdain for the meal. In all, I expected this to be a large part of the book when in reality it was only 300-400 pages worth of the book. While the rest of her life was definitely worth writing about, it seems like the author could have spent more time on this subject as it is one of the more well known parts of her life. The return back east lasted only a couple of chapters and didn't seem to give as much depth as everything else.<br /><br />Upon her return from the expedition they settle peacefully in St Louis where Clark's wife teaches her to sew and embroider and they have no worry of starving in the lean winter months (something that is shown quite prevalently in other parts of the books when she is with her native Indian tribes).<br /><br />One day, when the beatings from Charbonneau finally push her to the breaking point, she packs up her belongings and leaves and her ten year old son Baptiste stays with his father. She is taken in by a tribe of Comanche and remarries. Over the course of 26 years she has an additional five children, but only two out of them survive childhood.<br /><br />When her husband dies she leaves and seeks out the white man, hoping to find her first born son. The rest of the book follows this journey until she's well into her eighties and has settled down with her daughters and grandchildren.<br /><br />Sacajawea faced many hardships and Waldo's book explores many of them. It also faces her triumphs and her sorrows and really makes you believe you know everything she went through and can take a real peek at her life. Waldo also did a wonderful job of incorporating quotes and citations from numerous journals of the time at the beginning of each chapter. It provides factual background that helps make this fictional telling more believable. Each chapter starts out with an excerpt and she bases the next chapter loosely upon that excerpt, creating a story line for each chapter within the story itself. Her writing itself is very detailed and she seems to put a lot of emotion behind her words. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17020239895140922810" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_920528521" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/920528521" />
    <a title="Kathryn Bashaar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/994755-kathryn-bashaar"><img alt="Kathryn Bashaar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600110280p2/994755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/920528521?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn Bashaar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn Bashaar" href="/user/show/994755-kathryn-bashaar">Kathryn Bashaar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer920528521" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16708968201910732540">I read this book 30 years ago and loved it. So, when I saw it was the May choice of the Historical Fictionistas, I was excited to re-read it. I didn't like it as much on the re-reading. I'm disappointed to discover that, along with everyone else in the world, my attention span has been eroded by the internet and a long, detailed book is hard for me. Also, I think this book has a Mississippi delta of a plot: meandering, sprawling and muddy. The first half, describing the L&C Expedition, is pretty</span>
  <span id="freeText16708968201910732540" style="display:none">I read this book 30 years ago and loved it. So, when I saw it was the May choice of the Historical Fictionistas, I was excited to re-read it. I didn't like it as much on the re-reading. I'm disappointed to discover that, along with everyone else in the world, my attention span has been eroded by the internet and a long, detailed book is hard for me. Also, I think this book has a Mississippi delta of a plot: meandering, sprawling and muddy. The first half, describing the L&amp;C Expedition, is pretty tight, but the second half is all over the place. It felt like Waldo couldn't bear to leave out one little bit of her historic research, so she places Sacajawea, Forrest-Gump-like, in the middle of pretty much every single thing that happened in the American West between 1803 and 1875. Instead of good dramatic scenes, there's way too much "then this happened then this happened then this happened" exposition. <br />But I stuck with it, and there were some things that I still like about this book. I liked the vivid detail about Native American life. I was in Chicago at a conference for a few days while I was reading it and ate a $154 dinner and couldn't help thinking how excessive that was - when less than 200 years ago the majority of the residents of the Midwest were near starvation every winter.<br />And I love the character of Sacajawea, so worthy and dignified even in the most difficult circumstances - and, in her old age, so wise. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16708968201910732540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_40416578" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40416578" />
    <a title="John Clements" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1769421-john-clements"><img alt="John Clements" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228265367p2/1769421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40416578?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Clements" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Clements" href="/user/show/1769421-john-clements">John Clements</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40416578" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3179148521910411906">If you're up to reading this book get settled in and just accept that you're about to begin a very fruitful journey. Thoroughly researched and annotated, Waldo's SACAJAWEA is a historical epic worthy of being studied in high school history classes. But don't let that color your expectations, because this novel is also a sweeping tableaux of emotions and humanity. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1372389907" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1372389907" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5778052-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493898986p2/5778052.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1372389907?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/5778052-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11801963-sacajawea">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1372389907" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2512466182742297858">This book was simply amazing and I recommend it to anyone who likes to see history come alive! It was a very long book; however, I was totally engrossed throughout the book. Sacajawea led a very extraordinary life through her experiences with the Lewis and Clark expedition that taught her so much more than other Indian women of the period. She learned many languages and lived with several Indian tribes as well as with white explorers. The book is extremely well-written and, although it is an his</span>
  <span id="freeText2512466182742297858" style="display:none">This book was simply amazing and I recommend it to anyone who likes to see history come alive! It was a very long book; however, I was totally engrossed throughout the book. Sacajawea led a very extraordinary life through her experiences with the Lewis and Clark expedition that taught her so much more than other Indian women of the period. She learned many languages and lived with several Indian tribes as well as with white explorers. The book is extremely well-written and, although it is an historic novel, many facts are woven into the story (duly footnoted so you can see the inspiration for the story and read for yourself the actual evidence available). This book had to have been researched intensively. Anna Lee Waldo brought Sacajawea to life and this book seems a tribute to her life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2512466182742297858" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194095612" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5954350-l-maybe-you-can-have-too-many-books?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5954350-l-maybe-you-can-have-too-many-books?shelf=big-ass-book-challenge">big-ass-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5954350-l-maybe-you-can-have-too-many-books?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194095612" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3753382206072123801">I guess it isn't technically fair that I rate this book as I didn't finish it. I didn't even get to the part where Sacajawea joins Lewis and Clark, which was the whole reason I wanted to read this book in the first place. But I simply couldn't get caught up in the story as Anna Lee Waldo wrote it. I never found myself really caring for Sacajawea or what was happening to her. I'm still on the look out for a good book about this woman, but this thick novel is not it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

It set the stage for an awesome adventure with Sacajawea. <br/>The story is still wonderful all these years later.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_388770765" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388770765" />
    <a title="Mitzi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2946575-mitzi"><img alt="Mitzi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578006403p2/2946575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388770765?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mitzi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mitzi" href="/user/show/2946575-mitzi">Mitzi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2946575-mitzi?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388770765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10079614926902822557">I felt like I finished a marathon when I finally closed this book. I really wanted to like it - I admire the scope of it, the amount of research that was involved, and normally I enjoy reading really long books. Sadly, this book became a chore for me about 1/3 of the way through - I almost gave up on it multiple times. I didn't though, mostly because I almost never quit reading a book before the end. It kind of became a challenge to actually finish the damn thing!<br/><br/>So what kept me from enjoying it</span>
  <span id="freeText10079614926902822557" style="display:none">I felt like I finished a marathon when I finally closed this book. I really wanted to like it - I admire the scope of it, the amount of research that was involved, and normally I enjoy reading really long books. Sadly, this book became a chore for me about 1/3 of the way through - I almost gave up on it multiple times. I didn't though, mostly because I almost never quit reading a book before the end. It kind of became a challenge to actually finish the damn thing!<br /><br />So what kept me from enjoying it? The writing style is super clunky, with sentences and paragraphs sprinkled throughout that don't even make any sense. The pace is slow, to say the least. The emotional highs and lows are few and far between - in fact I can't say I really felt anything while reading it, other than some surface "oh I should feel sad here" moments that popped up once in a while. <br /><br />I'm guessing there are lots of other, better books about Sacajawea out there, I will probably try to track them down. The history itself was interesting - I would love to read about it in a well written book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10079614926902822557" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Growing up you hear the story of Sacajawea in school but this book really puts a face to the legend. You really see things through her eyes and see what a hard journey it was. I would definitely recommend this book. Be sure to allow some time to finish it as it is over 1400 pages long -- but well worth the read.</span>

<div id="review_70139433" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/70139433" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2301204-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70139433?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/2301204-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70139433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17192474076208035147">This is my favorite book of all time. It's the story of the Lewis and Clark Expedition from Sacajawea's point of view. Historically there are two theories about what happened to her after the expedition. One claims she died several years later, the other says she lived to a ripe old age and died in the late 1800s. This story takes the second claim and imagines what her life would have been. Excellent read for any historical fiction fan.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1720005470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1720005470" />
    <a title="Emily Carroll" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37247238-emily-carroll"><img alt="Emily Carroll" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1416768614p2/37247238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1720005470?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily Carroll" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily Carroll" href="/user/show/37247238-emily-carroll">Emily Carroll</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1720005470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17778233503482047353">I have owned this book for years and never got around to reading it until now. Looking at it, it's a pretty intimidating endeavor to take on. You have to imagine that it will likely take at least a month of commitment. I got lucky though, because "Sacajawea" seriously sucks you in right away and doesn't let go throughout the entire thing. It being 50+ chapters and 1,400 pages, it really has too or no one would finish it. Let alone, it becoming one of the top best sellers in the country and spend</span>
  <span id="freeText17778233503482047353" style="display:none">I have owned this book for years and never got around to reading it until now. Looking at it, it's a pretty intimidating endeavor to take on. You have to imagine that it will likely take at least a month of commitment. I got lucky though, because "Sacajawea" seriously sucks you in right away and doesn't let go throughout the entire thing. It being 50+ chapters and 1,400 pages, it really has too or no one would finish it. Let alone, it becoming one of the top best sellers in the country and spending 8 months on the New York Times best sellers list. I found that after finishing a chapter or two, I would continue to play the movie in my head and think about the last scene I read. I constantly wanted to read and couldn't wait to find out what would happen next. This book truly tells the full life story of the legend, who was Sacagawea, from girlhood to death and everything in between. Sacajawea's life and story began before she was given that name, when she was still known as Boniva, the curious daughter of a powerful Shoshone chief with a loving family. She lived a somewhat tragic life, that was always changing. As a young girl, she was brutally captured and her family was killed, she became a slave. During her long life, she was abducted, stolen, sold, traded, married off...Sacajawea hardly new consistency in both location or status. While reading this book, I tried to imagine how life was like and what Sacajawea would have seen and experienced and Waldo does a great job at giving enough descriptive images that you can do this. I felt as though I could truly imagine the pain and fear she was experiencing during the hard times, and the joy and love she felt during the good times. A common theme that I noticed was that Sacagawea rarely had the option to make her own choices, regardless of how capable she was. Women being considered objects or less then men seems to have been nearly universal throughout history in many cultures and regions, the natives if North America included. This is also what made Sacajawea famous in a sense. She was traded to and married a Canadian-French man named Toussaint Charbonneau. It was he, who gave her the ability to join the Lewis and Clarke Exbidition, which led to the fame she is well known for. The common perception of Sacagawea in the Lewis and Clark exhibition is that she led them on the trail, but in this book, she's never the leader, she follows the trail and helps along the way. I really appreciate that Waldo includes excerpts from actual letters and journals from the exhibition, which she based the book on, so you see that along with her own creative elements, the reality and truth behind it. This book has definitely made it into my top favorites and I highly recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17778233503482047353" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58051414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8858656758768482848">Fabulous historical fiction! Waldo spent many years reasearching to write this book and includes copious notes, which solidify the actual history and give the reader a sense of why the fictional parts weave the way they do. Growing up in Portland, Oregon, I was taught about Lewis and Clark in school, but it came nowhere near the depth and breadth of Waldo's research. <br/><br/>Sacajawea's story runs deep: as an Indian, a woman, an intrepid traveler, a lover of peace. As I approached the last of the 1328 p</span>
  <span id="freeText8858656758768482848" style="display:none">Fabulous historical fiction! Waldo spent many years reasearching to write this book and includes copious notes, which solidify the actual history and give the reader a sense of why the fictional parts weave the way they do. Growing up in Portland, Oregon, I was taught about Lewis and Clark in school, but it came nowhere near the depth and breadth of Waldo's research. <br /><br />Sacajawea's story runs deep: as an Indian, a woman, an intrepid traveler, a lover of peace. As I approached the last of the 1328 pages, I was truly filled with melancholy. I wanted to know the wonderful ending, but I didn't want the story to end! <br /><br />Waldo really did a wonderful job making the many facets of Sacajawea's life so real to me that I felt a great kinship with her by the middle of the great Expedition, which ended about a third of the way through this book. <br /><br />For those history buffs, many points along the Expedition's trail are pointed out, including what Lewis and Clark named them and what their current names are. Many references to the diets of different Native tribes were a bonus for me. Covering from about 1800 to 1900, there is a wealth of knowledge on the daily lives of early Americans. <br /><br />This is a rare novel for me - I WILL read it again! </span>
  <a data-text-id="8858656758768482848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Janith Pathirage" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32306993-janith-pathirage"><img alt="Janith Pathirage" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540403523p2/32306993.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3316335452?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janith Pathirage" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janith Pathirage" href="/user/show/32306993-janith-pathirage">Janith Pathirage</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3316335452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4669898012792913519">Historical fiction has introduced me some gems over the past few years and this was one of the finest books I came across so far! Part history and part fiction, Sacajawea is the story of a great woman who had to go through more headship in her life than Scarlett O'Hara ever did. This 1400 page saga is highly educational if you can combine it with bit of Google research to separate facts from fiction. Only thing I didn't like about this book was the crime Sacajawea committed. She's not the type w</span>
  <span id="freeText4669898012792913519" style="display:none">Historical fiction has introduced me some gems over the past few years and this was one of the finest books I came across so far! Part history and part fiction, Sacajawea is the story of a great woman who had to go through more headship in her life than Scarlett O'Hara ever did. This 1400 page saga is highly educational if you can combine it with bit of Google research to separate facts from fiction. Only thing I didn't like about this book was the crime Sacajawea committed. She's not the type who takes someone's life under any circumstance. That was very character damaging and sloppy work from Anna Lee Waldo, she could have played it in so many different ways with her creativity. <br /><br />Apart from that, I had a really good time reading this novel. I was watching 'Bury my Heart at Wounded Knee' yesterday when I was just about to finish reading the book and to my surprise, both stories crossed each other. That was a nice finishing touch for the book. This novel deserves more recognition than what it actually has.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4669898012792913519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1110736365" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1110736365" />
    <a title="Jaksen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen"><img alt="Jaksen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455934399p2/7233842.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1110736365?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaksen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaksen" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen">Jaksen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1110736365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7394349970772039582">Read this book shortly after it came out.<br/><br/>This was what I call a 'discovery book.' I discovered it at the family summer cottage being used to hold up a window. (The window's rope pulleys had snapped, and the weights which keep the window up - or down - were permanently stuck in the window frame. Okay, it's an old family cottage.)<br/><br/>So the poor book, whose purpose was to keep the window from smashing down on someone's hands, was rain-stained, and some of its pages were tattered. But I liked the cover</span>
  <span id="freeText7394349970772039582" style="display:none">Read this book shortly after it came out.<br /><br />This was what I call a 'discovery book.' I discovered it at the family summer cottage being used to hold up a window. (The window's rope pulleys had snapped, and the weights which keep the window up - or down - were permanently stuck in the window frame. Okay, it's an old family cottage.)<br /><br />So the poor book, whose purpose was to keep the window from smashing down on someone's hands, was rain-stained, and some of its pages were tattered. But I liked the cover - and the subject - and read it one rainy weekend back in 1978 or '79. (I still have it, in the cottage bookcase. It's now just a book, not a window-holder.)<br /><br />It must have been my aunt's, I'm guessing. (I owe a lot, literary-speaking to that aunt.) Anyhow, I just kept reading. I had no kids, yet, but I did have a fairly new husband, but I kept reading. Inside, outside, at the beach, in the car...<br /><br />The story is long and complex. As one reviewer had said, this writer left nothing out. If she learned something new about Sacajawea, she put it in. But if I were editing it, I'd be hard put to decide what didn't need to be in there. The details of native American life, juxtaposed with American life at that time, is for me, just perfect. And the way S. balanced her life with constantly changing and different expectations from: her family, the men in her life, and then the white men. Exceptional woman.<br /><br />Great book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7394349970772039582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/218699908?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tori" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tori" href="/user/show/5834658-tori">Tori</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5834658-tori?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer218699908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17982673158782168749">It has been some time since I've read this book that I'm writing this, but I just want to say amazing. In my top 10 favorites list for sure.<br/>I have always been sort of interested in Sacajawea, but there's not MANY facts about her out there. This novel, however, really brought her to life more than anything else to me. Now I am more absorbed in her past as I was before.<br/>I will be re-reading this book. It was a fantastic read, and it really inspired me.<br/>All from Sacajawea's fictional point of view, i</span>
  <span id="freeText17982673158782168749" style="display:none">It has been some time since I've read this book that I'm writing this, but I just want to say amazing. In my top 10 favorites list for sure.<br />I have always been sort of interested in Sacajawea, but there's not MANY facts about her out there. This novel, however, really brought her to life more than anything else to me. Now I am more absorbed in her past as I was before.<br />I will be re-reading this book. It was a fantastic read, and it really inspired me.<br />All from Sacajawea's fictional point of view, it is easy to imagine that she was actually telling the story and events herself (even though of course she wasn't).<br />But it does showcase her bravery, hardships, and what her life must have been like. Such a courageous woman, and truly one of the best historical mothers out there. Because we do know they indeed she led Lewis and Clark all the while with a baby on her back. Assuming how things were back in her years I'm sure she did it with very little or no complaining, and it's just an inspiring book. I felt as if I was there with her the whole entire time, and like this really WAS her life. This fictional story was just amazing. I recommend it to all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17982673158782168749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WjvRAxDaZJWXWajv/HJHtRYhFrFFQcFUE0Rywx0WSWmxm8zjiSM1nxA/E5XT5N9zoyaT+bAxgL3Ztc0+vdwYiw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

She said he had lived across the ocean near what he called the &quot;Kamby Bolongo&quot; and had been out in the forest one day chopping wood to make a drum when he was set upon by four men, beaten, chained and dragged aboard a slave ship bound for Colonial America.\n\nStill vividly remembering the stories after he grew up and became a writer, Haley began to search for documentation that might authenticate the narrative. It took ten years and a half a million miles of travel across three continents to find it, but finally, in an astonishing feat of genealogical detective work, he discovered not only the name of &quot;the African&quot;—Kunta Kinte—but the precise location of Juffure, the very village in The Gambia, West Africa, from which he was abducted in 1767 at the age of sixteen and taken on the Lord Ligonier to Maryland and sold to a Virginia planter.\n\nHaley has talked in Juffure with his own African sixth cousins. On September 29, 1967, he stood on the dock in Annapolis where his great-great-great-great-grandfather was taken ashore on September 29, 1767. Now he has written the monumental two-century drama of Kunta Kinte and the six generations who came after him—slaves and freedmen, farmers and blacksmiths, lumber mill workers and Pullman porters, lawyers and architects—and one author.\n\nBut Haley has done more than recapture the history of his own family. As the first black American writer to trace his origins back to their roots, he has told the story of 25,000,000 Americans of African descent. He has rediscovered for an entire people a rich cultural heritage that slavery took away from them, along with their names and their identities. But Roots speaks, finally, not just to blacks, or to whites, but to all people and all races everywhere, for the story it tells is one of the most eloquent testimonials ever written to the As the classic story, first told over half a century ago, moves forward, the greatest love affair in all fiction is reignited; amidst heartbreak and joy, the endless, consuming passion between Scarlett O\'Hara and Rhett Butler reaches its startling culmination. Rich with surprises at every turn and new emotional, breathtaking adventures, Scarlett satisfies our longing to reenter the world of Gone With the Wind, and like its predecessor, Scarlett will find an Published in the spring of 1936, Margaret Mitchell\'s Gone With the Wind was an immediate and overwhelming success; millions of copies were sold in its first year alone. By the time the film opened on December 15, 1939, the anticipation and excitement were so great that the city of Atlanta declared the day an official holiday. <span id="freeTextContainer5119637273292155097">Anna Lee Waldo wrote the best-selling historical novel, SACAJAWEA. Her interest in the subject began as a child when she collected spear points on the shores of Whitefish Lake in Montana and listened to stories of Blackfeet and Crow grandmothers.<br/><br/>It took her ten years to write about the first woman to go with a military contingent, with a baby in a cradleboard, half way across the North American co</span>
  <span id="freeText5119637273292155097" style="display:none">Anna Lee Waldo wrote the best-selling historical novel, SACAJAWEA. Her interest in the subject began as a child when she collected spear points on the shores of Whitefish Lake in Montana and listened to stories of Blackfeet and Crow grandmothers.<br /><br />It took her ten years to write about the first woman to go with a military contingent, with a baby in a cradleboard, half way across the North American continent. Anna Lee is now writing a sequence of books that began in Wales in the twelfth century called the DRUID CIRCLE series. These books are based on the elusive history of the son of Prince Owain Gwynedd, named Madoc, who came to America in 1170.</span>
